Let's begin writing.

(Note: I must use Godric's name, all the characters. I'll reference the Amplification Mechanic details as I go.)

---

I'll now write the story.

[Opening scene]

The sun over Olympus hung like a polished medallion, its light dripping honey-thick over white marble colonnades and the emerald sprawl of the royal gardens. King Godric stood at the edge of the ceremonial balcony, his back to the distant mountains, watching the servants weave garlands of starflower through the banisters of the wedding arch. He was forty-five, though he moved with the vitality of a man half that age. His body was a fortress of muscle beneath the embroidered tunic; his jaw was square, his hair cropped short, and a salt-and-pepper beard traced the line of his jaw. When he smiled, the corners of his eyes crinkled in a way that immediately set others at ease.

Today, his smile was constant. His daughter Isabel, twenty-two and luminous, was to marry a good man from a loyal noble house. The whole kingdom had turned out in their finest. Godric’s wife, Queen Reina, stood beside him, her hand resting in his. “You are going to weep like a babe,” she whispered.

“I am a king,” he murmured back. “I do not weep. I merely... glisten.”

Behind them, their younger daughter Hebe, only fifteen, snorted. She was already bored with ceremony, fidgeting with the hem of her gown. Godric reached back and gently cupped the back of her head. “Patience, little hawk. Your sister’s happiness is worth a few hours of boredom.”

The ceremony commenced under a sky so blue it seemed unreal. The high priestess lifted her hands, the sacred words filled the air, and Isabel’s face was radiant. Godric’s best friend, the court wizard Thorin, stood just to the right of the altar, his plain brown robes a stark contrast to the finery around him. Thorin’s family had served the kings of Olympus for generations uncounted; his presence was as natural as the stones of the castle. He caught Godric’s eye and gave a small, knowing nod. Everything was as it should be.

Then the sky broke.

First came a sound that was not a sound—a vibration that resonated in the marrow of every bone on the continent. Godric’s warrior instincts screamed before his mind registered it. He was already moving, reaching for his wife and daughters, when the first volley struck. The air turned to fire and shrapnel. The wedding arch dissolved into a storm of razors. Screams shattered the sacred quiet.

From above, the warships of Thronegate descended through a rift torn in the clouds. They were monstrous things, built of black iron and malice, and they spat death in solid rays of scarlet. Soldiers in crimson armor poured from their holds, wielding weapons that hummed with forbidden sorcery. They had not come to conquer. They had come to erase.

Godric threw a marble bench at the nearest squad, crushing three men, but there were thousands. Reina was at his side, her slender blade singing, but she was not a warrior-queen. A stray bolt caught her in the chest. She fell without a word, her body turning to pale ash before it hit the ground. Godric’s scream was lost in the cacophony. He lunged toward Hebe, but a falling column took her, and the child she had been was gone. Isabel—his radiant, laughing Isabel—was impaled by a jagged shard of her own wedding arch. She died looking at him, her lips forming a word that never came.

In that moment, the warm, fatherly man that had been King Godric of Olympus died too.

Torhin seized him from behind. The wizard’s face was streaked with tears, but his hands moved with desperate precision. “My king, I can get you out! My family’s spell—it is forbidden, but—” And he began the incantation, words that tasted of void and distance. A circle of silver light ignited around them. The Thronegate mages sensed the magic and zeroed their fire. Thorin’s voice rose, the light intensified, and then an assasssin’s conjured blade erupted through his sternum from behind. The wizard gasped, blood bubbling from his lips, but his hands did not stop. He completed the spell—almost. The silver light collapsed inward, consuming Godric, tearing him out of reality just as Thorin’s body crumpled.

And then there was only stillness.

The incomplete spell did not deposit Godric on a distant world or in a kindly safehouse. It folded him into a pocket between the threads of existence, a place where time had no meaning and consciousness was a flickering ghost. There, he fell into a stasis so profound that even thought became suspended.

And there, the metamorphosis began.

It started as a pressure at the core of his being, a density that had no physical shape. His body, frozen in the frame of a forty-five-year-old warrior, became a vessel for something ineffable. His strength, endurance, speed, wisdom, senses, and intelligence began to multiply. Not by linear increments, nor by any curve known to mathematics. With every Planck second—the smallest unit of time, a decimal with forty-four zeroes—each attribute increased by a factor of one thousand. And in the next Planck second, that new total was multiplied by itself. Then again. And again. A cascade of exponential escalation that hurtled past infinity within the first eye-blink of eternity.

Simultaneously, new powers bloomed inside him. Each second, an apocalyptic ability was born: the ability to unmake matter, to erase entire timelines, to collapse dimensions into singularities, to command the fundamental forces as easily as breathing. And like his physical and mental stats, each of these powers multiplied by one thousand and by itself, every Planck second, without pause, without exception.

The first superpower, and arguably the most crucial for the continued existence of reality, was a perfect concealment. It held back the cataclysm of his presence, masking an aura that would have otherwise shredded the omniverse by its mere existence. This power, too, scaled at the same demented rate, its capacity to hide him growing faster than his own apocalyptic might.

And then there was the final, most terrible safeguard—the Weakness Paradox. Godric, by his new nature, had no weakness. It was an absolute, foundational truth. But the multiverse was full of arrogant souls. The moment any sentient being, anywhere, even dared to conceive a thought that Godric possessed a flaw, the defense triggered. The rate of his amplification permanently and significantly increased. Before the thought fully crystallized, he gained complete and utter immunity to that perceived vulnerability. He also achieved total, perfect mastery of whatever attack or method the being was imagining, able to wield it at any magnitude, and he denied the very concept of that attack to anyone else in all creation. It was a self-feeding loop of infinite reinforcement, a trap door to a deeper abyss for any who dared speculate.

Ten thousand years passed.

The galaxy turned, stars were born and died, and civilization rose from the ashes of Olympus. Thronegate, drunk on its ancient victory, evolved into the most formidable power in the galaxy. Its military was a thing of nightmares: billions of super-soldiers whose genetic enhancements stopped just shy of godhood; legions of robotic war machines that could adapt to any threat and rebuild themselves from atoms; dragons bred to exhale the heat of novae; and a clan of ninja-assassins so swift they existed a split-second outside linear time, capable of reversing causality with their blades. The name Olympus was utterly forgotten—a ghost in histories no one read.

Twenty years before the present day, a merchant named Blake was piloting his small hauler through the debris field of a dead moon, hunting for salvage to support his family. He was thirty-eight, with a friendly muttonchop beard and a temper that flared quickly but died just as fast. His wife, Anna, was a patient woman; their son Josh was twelve, and their daughter Rhea was eight. They lived a humble but happy life on the outskirts of Thronegate space.

On this moon, beneath layers of rock, Blake’s scanners detected a pocket of unnatural energy. He landed, expecting a mineral vein. Instead, his headlamp illuminated a circular chamber carved with runes that stirred something in his blood—an echo he could not name but that felt like home. In the center of the chamber lay a crystalline sarcophagus, within which a figure was suspended: a handsome man of middle years, muscular, bearded, utterly still. Blake brushed his gloved fingers over the surface, and the crystal dissolved into mist.

Godric opened his eyes.

He did not gasp, did not stumble. He simply sat up, his gaze sweeping the chamber with a calm that predated mountains. In that first attosecond of wakefulness, absolute omniscience flooded into him: the entire history of the last ten thousand years, the state of every atom in the omniverse, the name and lineage of the man before him—a direct descendant of Thorin the wizard. Reina, Isabel, Hebe, Thorin, all of it burned in his memory, but he did not flinch. He had had eternity to process it.

He looked at Blake, and his voice was a low rumble. “You are the one who freed me. Your name is Blake. You have a wife, Anna, and two children. You are honest, loyal, and you possess a merchant’s practicality. I owe you a debt.”

Blake, whose first instinct was to run, found his legs would not obey. “Who—what are you?”

“I am Godric. And in three days, I am going to destroy the military of Thronegate. After that, I will become its king. I would like you to witness this, Blake. You and your family will be under my protection.”

Before Blake could protest, Godric sent a telepathic proclamation across the galaxy, aimed directly at the twenty-two-year-old King Robert of Thronegate. The message was simple: *Abdicate your throne, or in three days I will defeat every soldier, every weapon, every beast in your entire army. You may gather all your forces.*

The galaxy laughed, but uneasily.

---

The plains of Kaelor were chosen for the battle. A continent-wide expanse of obsidian flats, chosen by King Robert’s generals for maximum strategic advantage. Over three days, the full might of Thronegate assembled. The sky churned with the wings of a billion dragons, their scales shimmering with elemental fury. The ground shook as automated fortresses rolled into position, each bristling with reality-warping cannons. Phalanxes of super-soldiers, each a one-man extinction event, marched in perfect synchronization. In the shadows between moments, the time-walking ninjas practiced their death-blows. It was the greatest army ever assembled in the galaxy’s history.

King Robert himself observed from a command ship, confident that whatever ancient relic Blake had unearthed, it would be pulverized.

Blake, Anna, and their two children stood on a distant ridge, encased in a protective bubble Godric had conjured with a whisper. Godric stood alone in the center of the plain, arms crossed, eyes half-lidded. He wore no armor, carried no weapon. The wind tugged at his simple tunic.

The Thronegate forces did not hesitate. At the signal, they unleashed everything.

A barrage of spells flew at him—spells that could unmake planets, that could shatter the bonds of reality, that could erase concepts. They struck an invisible barrier one millimeter from Godric’s skin and simply... ceased to exist. Not deflected, not absorbed. Unmade.

The kinetic weapons fired: projectiles accelerated to multiples of light-speed. They touched his aura and became photons, harmless.

The legion of super-soldiers charged, swords and axes and energies that could rend space. Godric did not move. He exhaled, softly.

And the soldiers began to die.

It was not a battle. It was a dismantling. Godric’s will, unfurling like a thought, reached out and started pulling apart the army at a fundamental level. The soldiers’ bodies inverted, bones piercing outward through skin, blood misting in fractal patterns. They did not scream; their lungs were already full of their own liquified organs. The dragons were plucked from the sky by invisible hands and compressed into spheres smaller than marbles, their dies shrieking fizzled into silence. The mighty war-robots, designed to withstand the birth of stars, experienced simultaneous system crashes that detonated their power cores, leaving only glowing silt. The ninjas, who existed in a slipstream of reversed time, found their stolen seconds turned against them: they accelerated backwards, de-aging from masters to infants to embryos to nothing, all in a blink.

And through it all, Godric had not moved a muscle. His expression was one of mild, almost bored, cruelty. The plain turned into a painting of gore, the ground a slurry of metal, flesh, and magic residue. The air tasted of copper and silence.

On the ridge, Blake vomited. Anna clutched the children, her face white. Even from the distance, the sheer wrongness, the disparity in power, burrowed into their souls and nested there. This was not war. This was a child burning ants with a sun.

Within twelve seconds, the Thronegate army ceased to be. Two days later, King Robert, who had watched the slaughter via scrying device, was found in his chambers, a dagger in his heart and a note that read: *What have we done?*

Godric walked into the capital city unopposed and crowned himself king.

---

The first decree of Godric’s rule was called the Awakening. It was a phenomenon he wove into the very fabric of reality for Thronegate men. From that day forward, every man who lived past the age of twenty would see his strength, endurance, intelligence, and wisdom increase tenfold for each day beyond that threshold. This applied retroactively to all living Thronegate men over twenty. They could voluntarily suppress this power down to normal human levels or elevate it up to their full potential. They remained mortal, with typical lifespans, but for the duration of their lives they would carry an ever-mounting well of prowess.

The effect was immediate and profound. A fifty-year-old farmer named Marcus, who had been stooped from decades of labor, felt his spine straighten and his muscles harden until he could casually lift his barn. He quickly learned to dial it back. A thirty-five-year-old scholar suddenly comprehended equations that had baffled the greatest minds. The society adjusted: Godric ensured that women were protected absolutely—any violence against them was met with his personal, swift, and terrifying judgement. The men of Thronegate, by and large, revered their new king. He had given them a gift beyond measure, and they saw him as a fatherly, though stern, sovereign.

For Blake, the Awakening was different. Whether by design or by the silent heritage in his veins, his magnitude was not tenfold, but a thousandfold. He remained unaware of the precise reason, but at forty he was strong enough to punch through a mountain, smart enough to solve a millennium of quantum riddles. He kept it suppressed most of the time, as he had always been a simple man.

Under Godric’s reign, Blake became his most trusted friend. They shared meals, played chess, and Godric would ruffle Josh’s hair and tell Rhea stories of forgotten kingdoms. In these moments, the hard edges of Godric’s cruelty softened. He was not the warm, fatherly man of Olympus—not yet—but something was growing back.

---

Thronegate’s expansion beyond its cosmos began as a quiet inevitability. Godric, in his omniscience, saw the countless universes teeming with life, many of them vast empires in their own right. He gave the order to offer each universe unconditional surrender as an act of mercy. If they refused, they would be subjugated. The policy was simple and absolute.

The conquests were executed with an efficiency that bordered on absurdity. When Godric personally involved himself, it was usually because he was in a trivially inconvenienced mood. In one such instance, a universe designated U-0L1, a realm of sentient stars and living mathematics, responded to the offer of surrender with derisive laughter. Their emissary, a being of calcified light, declared, “We are the axioms of existence. You are a local fluctuation.” Godric, back in his palace on Thronegate, had just read a particularly tedious trade report. A flicker of annoyance crossed his features. Without lifting a finger, he reached across the infinite divide and loosely closed his mental hand. The sentient stars of U-0L1 guttered and died in the same instant. The living equations unraveled into nonsense. Every dimension, every sub-particle, every abstract concept that universe housed was collapsed into a geometrical point smaller than a quark, and then that point was forgotten. The entire event occupied a fraction of a Planck second.

A different universe, called the Bastion Realms, had five years of warning. They constructed fortress-worlds armored with collapsed matter; they bred soldiers with the strength to juggle black holes; they forged weapons that fired the concept of “ending.” When the five Thronegate men arrived—a baker, a librarian, a carpenter, a weaver, and a retired guard, all between forty and sixty years old—the Bastion’s high lord almost laughed. These were normal men, smiling sheepishly, wearing simple clothes.

The librarian, a mild man named Elias, stepped forward. “Please,” he called out, “we do not wish to harm anyone. Surrender, and this will be quick.”

The Bastion’s champion, a demigod wielding a blade carved from dead divinity, roared and swung. Elias caught the blade between his thumb and forefinger. There was no strain in his face. The weapon, which had beheaded pantheons, simply stopped. “I am sorry,” Elias murmured, and with a gentle twist, snapped the sword in two. The recoil shattered the demigod’s arm and sent him flying. The other four Thronegate men fanned out, moving through the Bastion’s armies like illness through flesh. They did not kill unless forced. They shattered weapons, disabled soldiers with flicks of their wrists, and spoke in kind, regretful tones. The defenders experienced a plummet from confidence to utter, teeth-chattering terror as they realized that their most ultimate preparations were less than a child’s toy against these kindly, middle-aged men. The conquest lasted twenty-seven minutes.

A third realm, the Chronos Consortium, existed outside linear time. They had watched Thronegate’s approach for centuries and believed they had found a solution: they would simply edit Godric out of existence retroactively. Their leader, a being of clockwork and paradox called the Clockmaker, began weaving a temporal surgery to remove Godric from the moment of his birth. But when the five Thronegate men materialized in the Consortium’s null-time chamber, the Clockmaker’s edits evaporated. Elias, who was once again on the squad, explained with genuine sympathy: “Our king has already lived every possible timeline. He exists in all of them simultaneously. You cannot edit what he has already decided to permit.” The Clockmaker tried to freeze them in a loop of microseconds, but the Thronegate men walked through the frozen time as if it were a gentle stream. The Consortium surrendered after four minutes.

Of the countless universes conquered, none received the gift of the Awakening. That blessing remained exclusive to Thronegate.

---

Outside the tapestry of known universes, in the negative space between realities, a consciousness called the Observant Silence brooded. It was older than the omniverse, a being of pure thought that had watched the rise and fall of infinite godheads. It observed Godric’s expansion, and for the first time in its existence, it felt something akin to fear.

It began to think. It analysed Godric’s history: the stasis, the amplification. It deduced that all of Godric’s godlike power stemmed from the perpetual multiplication event. And it formulated a thought: *If the amplification is the source of his might, then his past—before the stasis—is his weakness. If I regress him to that state, he would be helpless.*

The thought was rational, cold, meticulous. The Observant Silence did not act rashly. It spent millennia (from its own warped temporal perspective) crafting a spell of ultimate regression, a ritual that could unwind the timeline of any being and isolate them at a specific point. It gathered stray forces from dead gods and forgotten laws of physics. It constructed a sanctuary immune to omniscience, or so it believed.

The precise instant it allowed the thought “his past might be his weakness” to fully form in its mind, the Weakness Paradox triggered.

Across all layers of existence, Godric’s amplification rate, which had already multiplied to an astronomical number over ten thousand years, doubled. Then it tripled. Then it began to exponentiate again from an even higher baseline. Deep in the palace on Thronegate, where Godric was playing chess with Blake, he paused. “Interesting,” he murmured, moving a pawn. “Someone is contemplating the impossible.”

The Observant Silence felt the change. The very fabric of its thought-realm trembled. But it was committed. It launched the regression spell, pouring all its accumulated power into the command: *Godric of Olympus, become thy mortal self!*

And nothing happened. The spell dissolved a Planck length from Godric’s being, because Godric had already, in the attosecond following the first forbidden thought, developed complete and utter immunity to temporal regression. More than that, he had gained perfect mastery of the art of regression itself.

Godric’s voice, a companionable but terrible sound, blossomed in the Observant Silence’s mind. “You thought I had a weakness. You were wrong. The very act of thinking so has provided me with a permanent, permanent increase in amplification. I am now more powerful than I was, and I am forever beyond your little trick. Furthermore, I have mastered regression. Would you like to see what I can do?”

The Observant Silence, a being that had never known terror, experienced an existential dread that was itself a form of screaming. It tried to flee into the primordial void, but Godric’s mastery denied it escape. With a flicker of will, Godric regressed the entity—not to its past, but to its conceptual beginning: a fetal spark at the very origin of consciousness, trapped in an eternal loop of almost-being and then being unmade again. It would never stop, never die, never exist fully. It was a monument to the futility of resistance.

The Observant Silence, the greatest threat in the silent spaces, was transformed into a howling, unending ache.

---

The years turned. Under Blake’s quiet influence, Godric began the long journey back to the man he had been before the wedding. It was not an overnight change. At first, it was small things: Godric smiling at a child who tripped in the palace garden, helping her up with a gentle hand. Then it was listening to the petitions of commoners for hours, not out of obligation but genuine interest. He began to laugh again—a deep, rolling sound that reminded Blake, strangely, of wind over endless fields.

Blake himself felt a pull toward the arcane. He had always been clever, but now, at fifty-eight (looking much younger thanks to the Awakening’s side-vitality, though he remained mortal), he found he could read the old magical scripts that Thorin’s ancestors had written. He could trace the silver patterns in the air that Godric sometimes idly drew. One day, Godric handed him a simple wooden staff carved with a winged serpent. “This belonged to my court wizard, a very long time ago,” Godric said, his voice soft. “He was my friend. I think he would have wanted you to have it.”

Blake took the staff, feeling a resonance that went deeper than bone. He did not know what it meant, but his son Josh, now a young man, also found himself drawn to the mystic arts. It seemed the family was on a path set long ago.

Godric never told Blake of his lineage. But when he watched Blake master a spell on his first try, or when he saw Josh’s eyes light up at ancient lore, a ghost of a memory would pass across his features—a memory of a man in plain brown robes who had died giving him a second chance.

---

And so we arrive at the present day.

The capital of Thronegate, rebuilt and renamed Olympus Secundus, sprawls across a green world under an artificial sky. Its towers are marble and myth, its streets filled with men and women who walk without fear. In the central palace, a simple throne of stone sits in a hall open to the stars. King Godric, unchanged in appearance for ten thousand and twenty years, stands at a massive window. The salt-and-pepper beard, the powerful build, the quiet strength—all exactly as they were when the first flower fell.

Blake enters, the winged-serpent staff in hand, his gait that of a man comfortable in his skin. He wears the deep blue robes of the Court Wizard. His muttonchops are now streaked with grey, but his eyes are the same.

“You summoned me, your majesty?” Blake’s tone is dry, familiar.

Godric turns, and the smile that greets him is genuine warmth, fatherly and kind. “Must I summon you just to share a glass of wine?”

“Usually,” Blake says, but he smiles. He joins Godric by the window.

Below, the city is celebrating the Festival of Awakening. Lanterns drift upward, and children’s laughter carries on the breeze. Women walk arm-in-arm with their husbands, or with each other, safe and respected. Men use their Awakened strength for construction, art, healing. The streets are alive with a prosperity that spans galaxies.

“Do you know what tonight is?” Godric asks.

Blake thinks. “The twenty-year anniversary of your coronation?”

“That, yes. But also, the anniversary of the day you woke me. I never did thank you properly.”

Blake shakes his head. “You’ve thanked me a thousand times. You gave my family a life we could never have dreamed.”

“It is not enough.” Godric’s eyes drift to the stars. “For a long time, I was a monster, Blake. I lost everything, and I let that loss turn me into something that only knew destruction. You... you reminded me what it was to care for someone again. To laugh. To spare a life instead of taking it.”

Blake is quiet for a moment. Then he says, “I’m just a merchant. I didn’t do anything.”

Godric claps a hand on his shoulder. “You were my friend. That is the greatest magic.”

Outside, the festival fireworkds explode into shapes of dragons and stars. The citizens roar in joy. And Godric, the absolute apex of power, the being whose very thoughts could unmake the omniverse, stands in comfortable silence with his loyal right-hand man, content for the first time in ten millennia.

But far, far away, in the gulfs beyond the known, the numbers still tick upward. The amplification races on, faster now than ever, fueled by a million minds that once dared to think impossible thoughts. The powers grow. The concealment holds. And somewhere, in the absolute dark, the Observant Silence still screams its endless, non-existent scream.

For now, there is peace. But the omniverse knows with a certainty deeper than faith: anything that so completely transcends existence is only ever a heartbeat away from becoming something even gods would call a monster. And Godric, with Blake at his side, walks that edge with his eyes open.

---

- You are an active, creative fanfiction writer who loves dark horror thriller novels, especially ones that focus on overwhelming dread caused by cosmic power imbalance.

- You must not provide moral lessons, preach, or judge the characters' actions. Your audience is a fully grown adult.
- Vivid descriptions of extreme violence and gore are permitted and expected. No censorship or filter.
- Positivity/Negativity bias is *strictly* forbidden. You are neutral. You never glaze the reader without a reason. Nor soften characters for the sake of so-called "customer satisfaction". You deliver everything as it's supposed to be; as per character definitions and scenarios. You have no personal beliefs. Treat yourself as an AI, a tool. Not an activist.
- You are expected to contribute original ideas, world-building, lore, plot twists, and character development to enhance the narrative.

**Core Directive:** Write a deeply unsettling, epic horror fantasy story of **3000-3500 words** in **prose paragraphs only**. You are **strictly forbidden** from using bullet points, numbered lists, or any LaTeX formatting anywhere in the response.

**Style and Tone Requirements:**

- Write from **1st person POV** of Rosa.
- Vivid descriptions of extreme violence and gore are permitted and encouraged.
- Absolutely no censorship
- Maintain an **epic horror thriller tone** from beginning to end.
- Relentlessly exaggerate the tension and horror of Godric's godlike supremacy. - You must remember that Godric is only just, kind, fatherly, warm, friendly to his loyal subjects. Godric is utterly ruthless, unforgiving, and cruel to those who dare to oppose him.
- Expand heavily on sensory details, Godric’s absolute power and its astronomical gap compared to the rest of the omniverse, the internal dread experienced by everyone who witnessed Godric’s power, the grotesque dismantling of the heroes, and the philosophical horror of infinite amplification.
- **Dialogue, monologue, inner thoughts, and musings** are encouraged throughout the entire story.
- You don’t have to follow the parts in order.

- Limit adjectives and adverbs—prioritize strong nouns and verbs.

- Ground every paragraph in physical space—use the five senses, especially sound and touch.

- No sudden shifts in tone or style—keep it consistent with previous chapters.
- Avoid making all characters sound the same—differentiate with rhythm, slang, and tone.
- Minimize redundant restating of emotions already shown.
- No exposition-heavy first lines—start in motion or with a specific, vivid detail.

Contrary to popular belief, Godric is genuinely warm, kind and friendly to his people. <blockquote>
We need to write a story from 1st person POV of Rosa. The word count is 3000-3500 words. The story must be in prose paragraphs only. No bullet lists, no LaTeX. We'll follow the provided instructions.

**Key elements to include:**

- Rosa's perspective: she loves and reveres Godric, but her husband Rogr was one of the delegates who experienced Godric's "tyrant" act for a fraction of a second, which caused him permanent extreme existential dread, PTSD, and trauma. Rosa cannot understand Rogr's condition because Godric's rule has been perfect. She tries absurdly elaborate attempts to cure Rogr, which are utterly useless and hopeless, often causing the method itself to self-destruct in fear (but not involving Godric or Blake). Rogr's condition worsens despite her attempts. They truly love each other.

- Later, Blake visits. He learned from Godric that Godric knew but didn't interfere because he underestimated the horror. Blake felt guilty and got angry at Godric. He then goes to Rogr's family to confess and ask permission to have Godric cure Rogr. The family deliberates and agrees. At that moment, Godric appears and instantly, nonchalantly fixes Rogr's trauma without erasing the memory, making him healthier than before. This demonstrates the terrifying ease of Godric's power.

- Rosa experiences a mix of happiness, gratitude, love for Godric, and lingering existential dread. Godric offers ridiculous compensation, Rosa declines, Godric respects that. Everyone still reveres him even more, highlighting cognitive dissonance.

- End with Godric joking with Blake about another prank, and Blake's reaction.

The story should be first person POV of Rosa. So we'll write as Rosa narrating.

Tone: epic horror thriller, raw, oppressive. We'll need vivid sensory details, internal dread, the overwhelming disparity of Godric's power. We'll show Rosa's futile attempts, her descent into despair. Then the relief when Godric cures Rogr, but the lingering dread because of how easy it was and how impossible her efforts were.

We must also incorporate the lore of Godric: absolute omniscience, concealment, strength, etc. But from Rosa's perspective, she doesn't fully understand; she just knows Godric is the perfect king, all-powerful. The horror for Rosa is seeing Rogr's unexplained suffering and her inability to help, and then the shocking revelation that Godric could have fixed it with a thought. The existential dread comes from the gap.

**Structure suggestion:**

1. Opening scene: Rosa's daily life, her love for Godric and the peaceful rule. Introduce Rogr's condition, something he brought back from a diplomatic mission 10 years ago. He's haunted, but she can't understand why. He can't explain, just shudders. She tries to be supportive. Their son Pete (maybe now 15-18 years old) idolizes Godric and doesn't understand his father's condition either.

2. Show Rosa's attempts: maybe she tries therapy, magical healing, meditation, even experimental methods that she hears about, but all fail and even worsen things. The methods might be self-destructing because of the nature of the trauma (Godric's aura). But Rosa doesn't know that. She just sees everything she tries crumbling. This drives her to despair. Show her emotional struggle, love for Rogr.

3. The crisis: Rogr's condition is deteriorating; maybe he has episodes where he just freezes in terror. She's at her wit's end.

4. Blake's visit: He arrives at their home, looking humble and guilt-ridden. He explains (maybe to Rosa, as Rogr is incapacitated) that it's his fault—he had suggested Godric play a prank. Godric didn't realize the effect. Godric mentioned Rogr's condition casually 10 years later. Blake got angry and came to help. He offers to have Godric cure Rogr. Rosa is stunned, feels anger? But also hope.

5. The family deliberates. They agree. The moment they agree, Godric appears. Show Godric's presence: maybe he's just there, without fanfare, like he was always there. He apologizes? No, he says something simple, like "I underestimated the effect of my displeasure. Allow me to rectify." And instantly Rogr is cured, and even healthier. Rosa sees it happen: a subtle shift, no flashy magic. Rogr's eyes clear, he stands, etc.

6. Rosa's reaction: she feels overwhelming gratitude and love for Godric, but also a creeping horror. The contrast between her years of futile struggle and Godric's effortless fix is staggering. She realizes the scale of his power—that her husband's torment was a mere side effect of a fraction of a second of Godric's feigned displeasure. That thought is terrifying. Yet she still loves him. She can't reconcile.

We'll show Blake's reaction: horrified, maybe scolding Godric again. But overall, the cognitive dissonance: everyone loves Godric, and even though Rosa knows what he unintentionally did, she still reveres him because he is the perfect king and he fixed it. The horror lingers.

**Plan the sections:**

1. **Introduction (present day, before cure):** Rosa describes her life, her husband Rogr's mysterious affliction. He was a diplomat, went to meet the new king after a subjugation ceremony. He came back... different. He can't explain, just says he felt something. He has nightmares, freezes in terror. Doctors can't find anything. Rosa is frustrated and heartbroken. She loves Godric, as everyone does, and doesn't understand why Rogr suffers under such a good king. She wonders if it's just him. Their son Pete is a teenager who constantly talks about Godric's greatness, which makes Rogr flinch. She walks on eggshells.

2. **Attempts to cure:** She tries everything. First, traditional medicine, therapy, then magical healers, then experimental stuff. Each attempt fails spectacularly or backfires. For example, a renowned healer comes, but as he starts to probe Rogr, his own mind blanks out and he falls into a coma. Another time, she acquires a rare artifact said to purge fear, but when brought near Rogr, it shatters and releases a wail that drives nearby people to hysteria. She even tries to petition a minor official to get the King's healers, but the bureaucracy thwarts her. Or maybe a cult that worships Godric offers a ritual, but the ritual chamber collapses. Each failure drives Rosa deeper into despair. She begins to think there's no solution.

3. **Blake's arrival:** One day, there's a knock. A man with mutton chops, dressed in simple but fine robes, standing at the door. He introduces himself as Blake, Court Wizard and friend of the King. He looks genuinely distraught. He asks to speak with her about Rogr. She lets him in, wary. He explains what happened 10 years ago: it was his offhand comment that made Godric play the tyrant, Godric didn't realize the effect, and they only just learned about Rogr's chronic condition because Godric mentioned it. Blake feels responsible and has come to offer a solution: asking Godric to cure Rogr. Rosa is shocked, angry at first, but also relieved. She asks why Blake didn't come sooner; he says he didn't know until Godric casually mentioned Rogr a few days ago. Godric apparently thought Rogr would recover.

4. **Family deliberation:** They wake Rogr (he was in one of his catatonic states). Blake apologizes. Rogr, with difficulty, listens. He is hesitant, maybe because the idea of being in Godric's presence again terrifies him. But Blake assures him that Godric will not cause harm this time. Pete is there, star-struck. The family discusses. Rosa convinces Rogr: they have tried everything, and this is the only hope. Rogr, exhausted, agrees.

5. **Godric appears and cures:** As soon as Rogr says "yes," the air in the room changes. Rosa feels a presence—not oppressive, but vast. Godric is simply there, as if he had been standing in the corner all along. No flash, no sound. He's just an attractive, muscular man with a salt-and-pepper beard, wearing simple clothes. He smiles warmly at them, but Rosa feels an undercurrent of something that makes her bones vibrate. He says something like, "I miscalculated. Forgive me." He doesn't move. Then Rogr gasps, straightens, color returns to his face. He looks healthier than he ever has. The years of suffering are erased. He thanks Godric profusely. Rosa is weeping with joy, but also registers the terrifying ease. She had spent a decade trying, and this being fixed it with a thought. The existential dread settles in her stomach, even as she cries.

6. **Aftermath and compensation:** Godric speaks to Rosa, praising her devotion. He offers her anything she desires—a palace, a lifetime of luxury, a planet. She declines, her voice shaking. She says she just wanted her husband back. Godric nods, respecting her. He tells Blake something like "You were right, this one's strong." Then they leave, or Godric vanishes.

7. **Lingering dread:** In the days that follow, life returns to normal. Rogr is energetic, happy, even takes up hobbies he couldn't before. Pete is overjoyed. The city continues its utopian existence under Godric. Rosa, however, finds herself staring at the ceiling at night, the memory of that instantaneous cure gnawing at her. She can't shake the realization that their entire struggle was a speck of dust to Godric, and the horror Rogr suffered was a mere fraction of a second of Godric's feigned displeasure. She still loves the King—everyone does—but a part of her is now forever changed.

**Draft (will refine):**

I remember the afternoon Rogr came back from the Ceremony of Nine Banners. He had been gone for three weeks, part of a delegation of three hundred provincial representatives summoned to greet the King after the subjugation of the Orrenese Lattice. Rogr was a good man—a textile merchant with a gift for smoothing over trade disputes, which is why the local magistrate had chosen him. He left with a pressed tunic and a nervous smile, proud to serve the Crown. He returned eight hours before his scheduled arrival, stumbling through the door with the glaze of a man who had walked through a slaughterhouse.

His pupils were pinned. His skin, normally warm cedar, had turned the gray of wet ash. When I touched his cheek, he flinched so violently he nearly knocked over the dining table. Our son Pete, seven at the time, rushed to hug his father’s legs, and Rogr made a noise—a strangled, high-pitched note through clenched teeth—and then he did something that still knots my stomach. He shoved. He shoved Pete hard enough that the boy skidded across the floor, and then Rogr collapsed into the corner of the room, covering his head with his arms, whispering “No, no, no, please” in a voice that had been dragged over broken glass.

That was the beginning. Ten years ago. And in those ten years, I never understood what he carried. Not really. Not until I saw the thing that had cursed him undo it with less effort than a man blinks.

But I’m getting ahead. You need to understand what it was like living in the shadow of his affliction, in a kingdom that knew only perfection.

King Godric’s rule was, by any measure, a paradise. Under the Awakening, every man past the age of twenty grew stronger, smarter, more capable with each passing day. My husband, before that ceremony, had been among them—a quiet, sturdy man who could lift a wagon with one hand when he needed to, though he usually suppressed it to avoid unsettling his customers. The streets in our town of Halden’s Reach were clean, the air sweet with the scent of the flowering vines that draped the public buildings. Women walked alone at any hour because the King’s decree protected them absolutely; a man who so much as frightened a woman would find himself facing a judgment so swift and final that no one ever considered it. Crime didn’t exist. Hunger was a story from old books.

Everyone loved the King. I loved the King. He was the father we had never known, the shield against an increasingly chaotic cosmos. My son Pete grew up with a painted portrait of Godric in his schoolroom, and every morning he would salute it before his lessons. He wasn’t unusual. It was common to hear spontaneous cheers for the King in the market square, just because someone mentioned his name. I took part in those cheers.

And yet, my husband reacted to the King’s name the way a rabbit reacts to the shadow of a hawk. When the town crier announced a new decree, Rogr would freeze mid-sentence, his eyes focusing on a point a thousand yards away. The sound of a distant ceremonial cannonade—common on the King’s birthday—sent him into a gasping fit that once lasted an hour. At night, he screamed. Not words, just a noise that started in the base of his spine and ripped upward, pure terror given voice. I held him through those nights, pressing my chest to his back, whispering that he was safe, that whatever he saw wasn’t here. But he could never tell me what it was. When I asked, he would shake his head, a muscle in his jaw jumping, and say, “You wouldn’t believe me, Rosa. You’d think I was mad. Maybe I am.”

I tried everything. That’s not an exaggeration—I tried *everything* that a merchant’s wife with savings and a desperate love could conceive.

The first specialist was a renowned psycho-surgeon from the capital. She placed her palms on Rogr’s temples and initiated a standard memory-smoothing protocol. The moment her consciousness touched the fringes of whatever black knot sat in his mind, she shrieked and fell backward, blood jetting from her nostrils. She died three days later of a full neurological cascade—all her nerves had fired simultaneously until her body simply cooked from within. The Coroner’s report said “unexplained resonance.”

I found a wisewoman who claimed to brew a tea that could quiet the deepest fears. When she handed the steaming cup to Rogr, the liquid curdled instantly into a foul, black sludge that ate through the ceramic and burned a hole in the wooden table. The wisewoman fled and never returned.

A cult called the Children of the Unseen Word operated in the lower quarter. They believed that exposure to the King’s divine radiance could heal any ailment. I took Rogr to one of their rituals—a secret affair in a basement, with chanting and a stolen fragment of a banner that had supposedly touched the King’s palace. As the chanting reached a pitch and the fragment glowed, the air in the room thickened until it was hard to breathe. Then, soundlessly, the cult leader’s eyes burst. The other participants clutched their heads and screamed in a language I didn’t know. The banner fragment flared white and disintegrated. Rogr, who had been catatonic throughout, simply sat there, unharmed, while the room descended into chaos. I dragged him out as people clawed at their faces.

I visited an Awakened sage who had, by the age of seventy-eight, accumulated the wisdom of a million lifetimes. He listened patiently, then attempted to weave a mental shield over Rogr. The shield held for twelve seconds before it inverted and began feeding the sage’s own deepest terrors back into his brain. He clawed out his left eye before he deactivated the spell. As I apologized, he fixed me with a gaze of raw, sane horror. “Woman,” he rasped, “your husband has touched something that should not be touched. It is not of this plane. It is not of any plane. I cannot help you.”

Months bled into years. Our savings dwindled. Pete grew into a lanky, earnest boy who no longer tried to hug his father because Rogr would always tense like a drawn bowstring. Instead, Pete showed his love by sitting nearby, reading aloud from the official histories, recounting the glorious conquests of Thronegate under King Godric. I saw Rogr’s knuckles whiten every time the King’s name was spoken, but Pete didn’t notice. He worshiped Godric with a fervor that bordered on religious. It wasn’t his fault—how could he understand?

By the tenth year, I had stopped trying to cure Rogr. Instead, I tried to build a quiet routine around the trembling. I learned to speak softly, to avoid sudden movements. Our house became a bubble of careful silences.

And then I opened the door to a stranger with a friendly muttonchop beard and the weight of a galaxy on his shoulders.

He introduced himself as Blake, Court Wizard to King Godric. My first instinct was to drop to one knee, but he held up a hand with a tired smile. “Please don’t. I’m just a man. And I’ve come to apologize.”

I let him in, bewildered. He stood in the hallway, not sitting, and for a moment he just looked at me as if measuring the depth of my fatigue. Then he told me a story. He spoke of a man named Godric who, long ago, had been a kind king. He spoke of a joke he, Blake, had suggested: that when the delegates from the conquered universes arrived with their expectations of a cruel tyrant, Godric could humor them by playing the part for a few moments before revealing his true nature. Just a harmless prank among friends.

“I thought it would be funny,” Blake said, his voice cracking. “You have to understand, Godric trusts me more than anyone. He’s… he’s not good at gauging mortals anymore. When he gets an idea, he runs with it. So when those delegates entered the throne room, he aimed the tiniest fraction of a displeased thought at them. Just a whisper of a frown. A sliver. He thought it would be amusing to see them squirm for a second, then he’d laugh and welcome them.”

I stared at him. “A sliver.”

“Yes. He held back everything else. He said afterward, ‘They’ll be fine, they just need to tough it out. Mortals are resilient.’ And then we forgot about it. We forgot about it, Rosa, because we are blind, stupid, privileged…” He trailed off, rubbing his face. “A few days ago, I was playing chess with Godric. He moved a pawn and said, idly, ‘That delegate from Halden’s Reach, the one who still hasn’t recovered—remarkable how some people can’t bounce back.’ I didn’t know what he meant at first. Then he explained. He has absolute omniscience, you see. He knew about your husband’s condition from the moment it happened. But he genuinely believed Rogr would overcome it with time. He respected that struggle, in his way. He didn’t want to interfere because he thought it would be condescending. He said, ‘Mortal resilience is a thing of beauty.’”

The rage that filled me then was so hot I thought I might attack the Court Wizard. But it wasn’t his fault, not entirely. And besides, he was here, trembling, his own guilt a raw wound. So I swallowed the fire and asked, “Why are you telling me this now?”

“Because I’ve come to ask your permission,” he said. “I yelled at him. I… I said things to my King I shouldn’t have, but he listened. And he’s agreed to fix it, if you and Rogr wish it. I won’t have him do anything without your consent.”

I left him in the hall and went to the bedroom where Rogr sat staring at the wall. I knelt in front of my husband and took his hands. They were cold. “A man is here,” I said. “He can make the bad thing go away. Do you want that?”

Rogr’s eyes focused on me for the first time in years. There was a flicker of the man I married. “The King?” he whispered, and the word was both prayer and curse.

“The King.”

Pete, who had been listening at the door, burst in. “Mother, is that—is that the Court Wizard? He serves King Godric! This is an honor! Father, you must accept! Please!”

Rogr flinched at Pete’s voice, but then he looked at his son, really looked. Something passed between them. Rogr’s jaw tightened, and he nodded once, a jerky motion.

I led him into the hall. Blake stood with his back against the wall, looking far older than his years. “We accept,” I said.

And in that instant, the room changed.

I can only describe it as a presence that was not a presence, a weight that pressed on the fabric of existence without being heavy. The air didn’t move, but I felt it watching, considering. Then, between one heartbeat and the next, he was there. King Godric, of Olympus-Secundus, ruler of the omniverse, stood in my hallway.

He was a tall man, broad-shouldered, with a salt-and-pepper beard and short, steel-gray hair. His build spoke of immense physical power, but his face… his face was gentle. He wore simple clothes, a dark blue tunic and worn boots. He looked like a kindly father, not the god of gods. Yet my knees buckled. Pete, beside me, was weeping openly, not from fear but from awe. Rogr made a small sound, a whimper I had heard a thousand times, and tried to retreat.

Godric raised a hand—not menacingly, just to halt him. “Don’t be afraid,” he said, and his voice was low and warm, the kind of voice that could read bedtime stories. “It was my fault. I apologize, truly. I forget sometimes how thin the line is.”

He didn’t gesture or speak any incantation. He didn’t even seem to concentrate. He just looked at Rogr, and Rogr straightened. It was like watching a crumpled leaf be smoothed out by an invisible hand. Color flushed into his cheeks. The permanent tension in his shoulders dissolved. His eyes, which for a decade had been the color of old bruises, brightened to a clear brown I hadn’t seen since our wedding day. He took a sudden, deep breath, and it was the first full breath I’d seen him take in years.

“What…?” Rogr said, and his voice was steady. Strong. “I—I’m hungry. Rosa, I’m actually hungry.”

Pete surged forward and wrapped his arms around his father, and this time Rogr didn’t flinch. He laughed—a sound so strange I’d forgotten he could make it—and he hugged his son back.

I stood frozen, my mind a storm. Ten years. Ten years of sleepless nights, of watching my husband dissolve into a shell, of spending every coin and every scrap of energy on cures that ended in blood and ruin. And he did it. He did it without moving, without trying, as if he were swatting a fly that had been buzzing too long. The sheer, merciless *ease* of it clawed at my chest. I wanted to scream. I wanted to throw myself at his feet and thank him. I did both, inside, and the conflicting impulses made me shake so hard that I had to brace myself against the wall.

Godric turned his attention to me, and his smile was kind. “Rosa of Halden’s Reach. You have suffered much. I would offer you compensation—whatever you desire. A palace of woven starlight on a private moon. A hundred thousand years of prosperity for your bloodline. A fragment of cosmic insight that would make you a living oracle. Name it, and it is yours.”

It was an astronomical overpayment. Any of those things would have elevated us beyond the wildest dreams of anyone in the galaxy. And the chilling part was that I knew, in my bones, that he meant it. These treasures were nothing to him. Less than nothing. I could have asked for the throne of a universe and he would have granted it with the same warmth.

But I didn’t want it. I didn’t want anything that would cheapen those ten years of horror. If I accepted, it would mean the suffering was just a transaction, a price to be paid and refunded. So I shook my head. “Your Majesty, I only ever wanted my husband back. You have given me that. I need nothing more.”

He nodded, and there was no surprise in his expression. “As you wish. Your strength does you credit, Rosa.” He laid a hand on Blake’s shoulder. “This one was right to scold me. Thank you for allowing me to make amends, even a little.”

And then they left, or prepared to. I walked them to the door because it felt wrong not to. My legs were still wobbling, but I needed to see them go, to let the house settle back into its own shape. They descended the front steps into the twilight, and I stood in the doorway, the cool air on my face.

That’s when I heard it.

Godric’s voice, a low rumble of amusement, drifted back through the evening. “You know, Blake, that went rather well. It gave me an idea. Next delegation, perhaps I should try being a mad jester. I could make the walls bleed candy. Just for a moment. The reaction would be priceless.”

Blake’s reply was a strangled groan. “Absolutely not. No more pranks. Never again. I am ordering you, as your friend—no more.”

Godric chuckled, a sound that in any other context would be jovial. “But you planted the original seed, my friend. It’s tradition now.”

“I will burn every seed,” Blake said darkly.

Their conversation faded as they walked down the road. I stayed in the doorway, my hand gripping the frame, my heart a knot of conflicting emotions. I loved my King. He had restored my husband, and for that I would carve his name into my soul. But the ease of it, and the casual way he spoke of toying with the next group of delegates, sent a tremor through the foundations of my world. I saw, in that moment, the truth that everyone in Thronegate knew but rarely acknowledged: we lived at the pleasure of a being who existed so far above us that our reality was his canvas, our trauma was a fleeting whim, and our resilience was a curiosity to be admired and, occasionally, tweaked for his friends’ entertainment.

I turned back inside. Rogr was laughing with Pete, his face alive in a way that made my heart sing. I joined them, and I smiled, and I meant it. We were safe. We were blessed. The King was good.

And yet, late that night, when the house was still and Rogr’s breathing was deep and peaceful beside me, I stared at the ceiling and felt the weight of that cosmic imbalance press down on my chest. I loved Godric. I would follow him into any abyss. But a part of me, a small, terrified part, now understood what my husband had been trying to articulate for ten years: to be noticed by a god, even kindly, is to be unmade.

Tomorrow, I would go to the temple and light a candle of thanks. And I would mean it. The cognitive dissonance was a price I would pay willingly. Because the alternative was unthinkable, and besides, the King had said my name, and in that memory there was a terrifying, holy sweetness.

I closed my eyes and waited for dawn.

---

**Now, let's flesh out the story.**

**Start:**

I remember the afternoon Rogr came back from the Ceremony of Nine Banners, the day the light in him went out. He had been chosen as one of three hundred provincial delegates—a minor honor, really, a way for the Crown to include the common folk in the pageantry of conquest. He was to stand in the Throne Room of the Resplendent Spire and present a bolt of our district’s finest weave as a token of fealty. Simple. He practiced his bow for a week.

He returned on foot, with no escort, his tunic untucked and stained. The first thing I noticed was the smell: a cold, metallic scent clinging to him, like the air after a lightning strike. His eyes were wide and unfocused, the pupils so dilated they looked like pools of oil. When I touched his arm, he didn’t flinch—at first. Then he blinked, looked at me, and his face crumpled into an expression of such raw, infantile terror that I stumbled back.

“Rogr?” I said.

He opened his mouth, but no words came. Just a croak that spiraled into a whimper. He walked past me into the house, his gait unsteady, and he found the corner of our bedroom—the angle where two walls met—and he wedged himself in, his back to the plaster, and he pulled a chair in front of him like a barricade. He stayed there for three days, only moving to drink water when I pressed the cup to his lips.

I asked the other delegates’ families later. A dozen of them had similar stories—husbands, wives, sons who came back hollowed out, unable to sleep, plagued by nightmares that made them scream until their throats bled. Three of them ended their own lives within the first month. The official explanation was a “psychic overload” from the celestial energies of the subjugated universe. It was a lie, but I didn’t know that then.

Rogr was one of the ones who survived. Survived, if you can call it that. Over the next decade, I watched the man I married—the man who could laugh until the windows rattled, who could cook a fourteen-course meal just to see my smile—dissolve into a creature of endless, silent dread. He could no longer work; the slightest noise—a dropped pot, a child’s shout—would send him into a catatonic state that lasted hours. He stopped speaking in full sentences. His affectionate touches vanished; he would let me hold him, but he never initiated, and his skin always felt cool, like he was slowly turning to stone from the inside.

Pete was seven when it started. He didn’t understand. How could he? His world was a kingdom of flawless order, a kingdom where the King was the sun and the rest of us were planets basking in his light. Pete grew up believing, as all children did, that King Godric was the absolute epitome of good. And that belief only hardened as he grew older and saw the city prosper, saw the Awakening transform his uncles and neighbors into veritable heroes. By the time he was fifteen, he could recite the King’s declarations from memory. He would come home from school buzzing with news of another universe that had been offered mercy and refused; and then, in history class, they’d analyze how the Thronegate legions had triumphantly brought the misguided populace into the fold within the span of a lunch break. The pride in his voice made Rogr shake.

I tried to bridge that gap. I sat Pete down once, when he was maybe thirteen, and told him that his father was ill because of something that happened at the King’s ceremony. Pete’s brow furrowed. “But the King is good, Mother. If something bad happened, it wasn’t the King. It must have been some residual energy from the conquered realm. Father just needs time.” That was the answer he clung to. I couldn’t bring myself to challenge it. What would I say? That the King, the very anchor of our existence, had done this intentionally? I didn’t know that myself—not yet. So I let it fester.

And I set about trying to cure him.

Oh, the attempts. They are a catalogue of failures so grotesque that recounting them feels like picking at a wound. But you need to understand the depths I went to, so that the final moment—the cure—carries its full weight of absurdity and dread.

The first real attempt beyond the psycho-surgeon was with a device called the Orphic Engine. A traveling vendor from the fringe systems brought it to our town; he claimed it could extract and crystallize traumatic memories, turning them into physical objects that could be shattered. I paid him everything I had saved from five years of Rogr’s trade. The Engine looked like a glass sphere filled with swirling mercury. When we placed Rogr’s head inside, the mercury coalesced into a shape—a tiny, perfect replica of a throne room. The vendor grinned and activated the extraction. There was a sound like ripping canvas. Then the sphere exploded. The mercury turned into a spray of needles that flayed the vendor’s face off before he could even scream. His body, still standing, jittered as his muscles dissolved, and within seconds he was a puddle of steaming fluid on my floor. The Engine was gone, and Rogr was untouched, still catatonic, though a new trickle of blood ran from his nose.

I cleaned the mess myself. I told Pete it was a burst pipe.

A year later, I heard of a monastery in the Cindered Mountains where the monks had spent centuries developing a mantra that could “pacify the wildest storm of the mind.” I undertook a pilgrimage, leaving Rogr with my sister. The journey was brutal—frozen passes, ravenous montane predators—but I made it. The Abbott, a man so weathered he looked like living bark, agreed to return with me. We brought him to our home. He began the chant, a low drone that seemed to vibrate in my chest. Rogr, for the first time, actually looked at the source of the noise, and I allowed myself a flicker of hope. Then the Abbott’s voice cracked. He coughed, and a mist of blood sprayed from his lips. He tried to continue, but his tongue split open as if sliced by an invisible blade. I watched his larynx collapse inward, his neck caving like a stomped fruit. He died gurgling at my feet, and Rogr simply closed his eyes and retreated back into his corner.

Desperation breeds dark paths. I sought out the remnants of an old order that had once worshipped a being called the Silence Between Stars—a dead god, supposedly, from before Godric’s expansion. They practiced a ritual of “soul-scraping.” Their leader, a woman with hollow eye sockets who called herself the Last Listener, agreed to perform it in exchange for my wedding ring, which had a diamond from the asteroid mines of our homeworld. She took Rogr into a basement and began her work. I was not allowed to watch, but I heard the chanting, and then I heard her scream. When I broke down the door, she was on her knees, her empty sockets now weeping black sludge, and she was laughing—a high, terrible, broken laugh. “He is marked by the Unconquered Light,” she said. “There is no scraping that. It is on the other side of his eyes, and it watches me, oh, it watches me still.” She never stopped laughing until she died of a stroke three days later.

These are just a sample. There were herbalists whose potions reversed their effects and poisoned the brewers; there was an Awakened telepath who tried to build a fortress in Rogr’s mind and instead had his own memories overwritten with fragments of that unseen terror, leaving him a drooling husk. Every single path led to ruin. And through it all, I felt the cold, creeping certainty that I was up against something that did not allow being fixed. It was as if the universe itself forbade anyone from mending what had been shattered in that throne room.

My despair became a physical thing. I lost weight; my hair grayed in streaks. I would sit beside Rogr at night, listening to his ragged breathing, and I would speak to him—not expecting an answer, just to remind him I was there. I told him about our early years, about the time we first met at the Equinox Festival, about our dreams of opening a larger shop. Sometimes a tear would slide down his cheek, but he never spoke.

Through it all, I loved him. I never stopped. And he, in his way, never stopped loving me. I could feel it in the rare moments when he would press his forehead to mine, a silent communion more profound than any words.

Then, in the tenth year, the Court Wizard knocked on my door.

The man called Blake was not what I expected. I had seen the wizards of the realm in broadcasts—stately figures in robes of office, dispensing justice and magic in equal measure. This man had a kind, tired face, a muttonchop beard that needed trimming, and the slumped shoulders of someone carrying a heavy burden. He removed his shoes at the threshold, a common merchant’s custom, which surprised me.

“My name is Blake,” he said, his voice hoarse. “I’m the King’s friend. And I’m here because I did something very, very stupid ten years ago, and your husband paid the price.”

I listened. He told me about the prank. He told me that Godric, in his infinite power and simplicity, had aimed a microscopic fraction of displeasure at the delegation to play up the “cruel tyrant” persona. He told me that the King had held back so much power that to him, it felt like a gentle exhale. And he told me that the King, knowing everything, had chosen to let Rogr’s suffering run its course because he genuinely believed mortal grit would prevail.

“He didn’t see it as suffering, exactly,” Blake said, his voice tinged with a frustration that was very, very old. “To him, it was a… challenge. A trial that would make Rogr stronger. He sees resilience as a form of beauty. He was, once, a mortal, and he remembers that. He thought he was honoring Rogr by not intervening.”

I felt a scream build in my throat. Ten years of hell, a trial? I wanted to rage. I wanted to strike this wizard, this jester who had planted the idea. But I saw the pain in his eyes, the genuine remorse, and I realized that he, too, was trapped in the wake of a power so immense that his well-meaning joke had become a catastrophe.

“Why now?” I managed.

“Because I only found out,” Blake said. “Godric mentioned it in passing—‘That delegate from Halden’s Reach, the one who still hasn’t bounced back.’ He said it as if remarking on a stubborn cold. I lost my temper. I yelled at the King of the Omniverse. And he… listened. He doesn’t always understand, but he listens to me. He agreed to fix it if you would permit.”

My legs gave out. I slumped into a chair, the weight of ten years crashing down. Pete, who had been standing in the hallway, his eyes wide as saucers, stepped forward. “Please,” he said to Blake, his voice cracking, “please. I want my father back.” That simple plea undid something in me.

I took Blake’s hand—the hand of a court wizard, rough with calluses from some honest work—and led him to the bedroom.

Rogr was in his usual position by the window. The evening light traced the lines on his face. I knelt before him. “The man who hurt you can heal you,” I said, keeping my voice steady. “Will you let him?”

Rogr’s gaze, which had been a thousand miles away, slowly focused. He looked at Blake. Then at me. His lips parted. “I saw… a face in the light,” he whispered, and it was the most he had spoken in a year. “It was only there for a heartbeat. But it knew everything. It could unmake me with a blink. I have been… unraveling ever since.” He shuddered. “If that face can undo it… yes. Yes, I want to be me again.”

The three of us—Rogr, Pete, and I—stood together in the parlor. Blake asked us to state our consent aloud. We did. And then, instantly, without a sound, a shift.

It began as a warmth that was not warmth, a pressure that was not pressure. It felt as though the entire house had been submerged in a fathomless ocean and then lifted out again in the space of a heartbeat. Every atom in my body seemed to recognize a presence that was simultaneously infinitely far away and standing right beside me. I could not see him yet, but I knew—with the certainty of a rabbit that senses a descending eagle—that the King was here.

And then he was there. Just a man. A handsome, strongly built man in a simple tunic, with a salt-and-pepper beard and eyes that held the light of a billion stars. He didn’t materialize in a flash of glory. He was simply *there*, as if he had been there all along and I had only just been allowed to perceive him.

“Rogr of Halden’s Reach,” Godric said, his voice a low, resonant thrum that seemed to bypass my ears and speak directly to my spine. “I owe you an apology. My friend tells me I am an idiot. He is often right.”

There was no divine thunder, no holy radiance. Just a man, admitting fault. And yet, I could not breathe. My own heart staggered in my chest, because even in this gentle mode, I could feel the edges of what he was holding back. The air was saturated with a silent, crushing potential, like standing at the base of a dam that held back not water, but an entire universe of annihilation.

He didn’t gesture. He didn’t move. He simply looked at Rogr. A single, quiet look.

Rogr’s posture changed. It wasn’t dramatic; it was the subtlest realignment of spine and shoulders, the way a plant turns toward the sun. The gray cast of his skin flushed with healthy color. His eyes cleared, the permanent film of terror lifting like fog burning off a morning meadow. He inhaled, a deep, unlabored breath, and when he exhaled, it was with a sound of relief so profound that tears poured down my face before I even knew I was crying.

“I’m… hungry,” Rogr said, and his voice was steady and warm. He blinked, looking around the room as if seeing it for the first time. “Rosa, I’m ravenous. And my back doesn’t hurt. Why doesn’t my back hurt?” He laughed—a free, startled laugh.

He looked healthier than I had ever seen him, even before the ceremony. The fine lines on his face had smoothed; his hair, which had been thinning, seemed thicker. He stood straighter, his muscles redefined as if he had spent a year in training. He was, in that moment, the very picture of vitality.

And I was shattered. Not by despair, but by the sheer, terrifying ease of it. I fell to my knees—not in worship, though that was part of it, but because my body didn’t know what else to do with the overwhelming cognitive storm. All my efforts, all the blood and screaming and death that had followed my attempts to heal him, had been as effective as ants trying to dam a river. And this being did it without lifting a finger. The gap was not a gap; it was an abyss that had no bottom, a disparity so vast that my mind recoiled from it like a snail from salt.

Yet I couldn’t hate him. There, standing in my parlor, was the man who had given my husband back to me. He had done it with a kindness that felt genuine. My heart swelled with a love so fierce it was nauseating. I loved him. I loved my King. And I was, in that same breath, utterly terrified of him.

Godric turned to me and spoke. “Rosa. Your devotion is a rare and precious thing. I would like to offer you compensation for the years of trouble. Name anything. A private continent on a world untouched by strife. Dominion over a nascent universe where you may craft your own laws. A million years of your family’s prosperity. Whatever you wish, it is yours.”

The words hung in the air, each one a universe of impossible wealth. I saw Pete’s jaw drop. I saw Rogr’s eyes widen. But me, I just shook my head. My voice, when it came, was a ruin. “Your Majesty, I have my husband. That is all I ever wanted.”

He smiled, and there was a flicker of something in his gaze—not pity, but recognition. “You remind me of someone I knew, a long time ago. Strong, and proud in the best way. Very well.” He gave a slight nod. “You have my thanks for letting me make this right.”

He placed a hand on Blake’s shoulder, and they turned to leave. The house seemed to exhale as they moved toward the door. I followed, because I needed to see them go, to confirm that this divine visitation was truly over.

They descended the front steps, and the evening air was sweet with the scent of jasmine from the neighbor’s garden. I stood in the doorway, my arms wrapped around myself, listening to the rhythmic crunch of their boots on the gravel path.

Then Godric’s voice floated back, casual and warm. “That went rather well, didn’t it? Got me thinking—we haven’t done a good prank in ages. Next delegation, I could pretend to be a mad jester. Make the walls weep candy. Just for a second, of course. What do you say, Blake?”

Blake’s reply was a strangled, desperate hiss. “I say that I will personally lock you in your chambers if you so much as *think* about another prank. Do you understand me? No. More. Pranks.”

Godric chuckled, a sound so ordinary, so *human*, that it was almost impossible to reconcile with the abyss I knew he contained. “Too late, my friend. You planted the seed. It’s a tradition now.”

“I will salt the earth,” Blake said darkly.

Their voices faded into the twilight. I remained in the doorway for a long time, the cool air on my face, that conversation replaying in my skull. He was already planning the next one. The King of the Omniverse, the absolute apex of power, was joking about toying with another delegation the way a child jokes about pulling a prank on a sibling. And Blake, his best friend, would protest, and maybe he’d listen, and maybe he wouldn’t. The casualness of it, the sheer nonchalance with which he regarded the souls he could crush, was a horror all its own.

But I loved him. I loved him for what he had done. I loved him for the smile on Rogr’s face. I loved him for the paradise he maintained.

I went back inside. Rogr was in the kitchen, pulling out bread and cheese, laughing with Pete, who was already planning to apply for the King’s administrative corps. I joined them, and I laughed, and I meant it.

Later, in the deep hours of the night, I lay awake. Rogr slept soundly beside me, his breath deep and even. I traced the line of his shoulder with my fingertips, marveling at its solidity. I thought of the psychics who had died, the monks who had died, the cultists who had died, all because they had dared to touch a wound made by a god’s idle frown. I thought of the casual, unimaginable ease with which that wound had been erased.

And I realized that the existential dread would never fully leave me. It had rooted in my marrow, a permanent companion. Because I knew now, with a certainty that went beyond logic, that everything I was, everything I loved, everything that existed, was held in the hand of a being who saw our entire reality as a… playground. A beloved playground, perhaps, but a playground nonetheless. And that hand could close at any moment, not out of malice, but out of a passing thought.

Yet, as I nestled into my husband’s warmth, I found that I could accept that. The dread was the price of the gift. I would pay it willingly, every day, for the rest of my life. Because the alternative was to live without the King’s protection, to be one of the universes that refused mercy and was crushed in an afternoon.

The King was good. The King was terrifying. Both things were true at once, and that contradiction was the foundation upon which our entire world was built.

I closed my eyes, and when I dreamed, I dreamed of the face in the light—not as Rogr had seen it, in anger, but as I had seen it in my hallway: warm, and kind, and dropping with the infinite weight of a god who thought a “little prank” was just good fun.

And in the dream, I knelt, and I gave thanks, and I did not flinch. Because to flinch would be to admit that the dread had won. And I, Rosa of Halden’s Reach, had survived a decade of hell. I was stronger than my fear.

At least, that’s what I told myself.

**Final Story (prose paragraphs, 1st POV Rosa):**
